<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">

<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/College_Football_All-Southern_Team" title="College Football All-Southern Team">All-Southern</a> (<a href="/wiki/1900_College_Football_All-Southern_Team" title="1900 College Football All-Southern Team">1900</a>)</li>

<p><b>George Catlett Marshall, Jr.</b> (December 31, 1880 – October 16, 1959) was an American statesman and soldier. He was <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Chief of Staff of the United States Army</a> under presidents <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, and served as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a> under Truman. He was hailed as the "organizer of victory" by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> for his leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> victory in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-a_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-a-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Uniontown,_Pennsylvania" title="Uniontown, Pennsylvania">Uniontown, Pennsylvania</a>, Marshall was a 1901 graduate of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Military_Institute" title="Virginia Military Institute">Virginia Military Institute</a>. After serving briefly as commandant of students at the Danville Military Academy in <a href="/wiki/Danville,_Virginia" title="Danville, Virginia">Danville, Virginia</a>, Marshall received his commission as a second lieutenant of Infantry in February, 1902. In the years after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish-American_War" title="Spanish-American War">Spanish-American War</a>, he served in the United States and overseas in positions of increasing rank and responsibility, including platoon leader and company commander in the Philippines during the <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">Philippine–American War</a>. He was the Honor Graduate of his Infantry-Cavalry School Course in 1907, and graduated first in his 1908 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Command_and_General_Staff_College" title="United States Army Command and General Staff College">Army Staff College</a> class.</p>
<p>In 1916 Marshall was assigned as <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">aide-de-camp</a> to <a href="/wiki/J._Franklin_Bell" title="J. Franklin Bell">J. Franklin Bell</a>, the commander of the Western Department. After the United States entered <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, Marshall served with Bell while Bell commanded the Department of the East. He was assigned to the staff of the <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="1st Infantry Division (United States)">1st Division</a>, and assisted with the organization's mobilization and training in the United States, as well as planning of its combat operations in France. Subsequently assigned to the staff of the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces" title="American Expeditionary Forces">American Expeditionary Forces</a> headquarters, he was a key planner of American operations including the <a href="/wiki/Meuse-Argonne_Offensive" title="Meuse-Argonne Offensive">Meuse-Argonne Offensive</a>.</p>
<p>After the war, Marshall was assigned as an <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">aide-de-camp</a> to <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">John J. Pershing</a>, who was then serving as the Army's Chief of Staff. He later served on the Army staff, commanded the <a href="/wiki/15th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="15th Infantry Regiment (United States)">15th Infantry Regiment</a> in China, and was an instructor at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_War_College" title="United States Army War College">Army War College</a>. In 1927, he became assistant commandant of the Army's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Infantry_School" title="United States Army Infantry School">Infantry School</a>, where he modernized command and staff processes, which proved to be of major benefit during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. In 1932 and 1933 he commanded the <a href="/wiki/8th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="8th Infantry Regiment (United States)">8th Infantry Regiment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tybee_Island,_Georgia" title="Tybee Island, Georgia">Fort Screven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>.</p>
<p>Marshall commanded 5th Brigade, <a href="/wiki/3rd_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="3rd Infantry Division (United States)">3rd Infantry Division</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Barracks" title="Vancouver Barracks">Vancouver Barracks</a> from 1936 to 1938, and received promotion to <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">brigadier general</a>. During this command, Marshall was also responsible for 35 <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a> (CCC) camps in Oregon and southern Washington. In July 1938, Marshall was assigned to the War Plans Division on the War Department staff, and he was subsequently appointed as the Army's Deputy Chief of Staff. When Chief of Staff <a href="/wiki/Malin_Craig" title="Malin Craig">Malin Craig</a> retired in 1939, Marshall became acting Chief of Staff, and then Chief of Staff. He served as Chief of Staff until the end of the war in 1945.</p>
<p>As Chief of Staff, Marshall organized the largest military expansion in U.S. history, and received promotion to <a href="/wiki/Five-star_rank" title="Five-star rank">five-star rank</a> as <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)#World_War_II_era" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a>. Marshall coordinated Allied operations in Europe and the Pacific until the end of the war; in addition to being hailed as the organizer of Allied victory by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine named Marshall its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Magazine_Person_of_the_Year" title="Time Magazine Person of the Year">Man of the Year</a> for 1943. Marshall retired from active service in 1945, but remained on active duty, a requirement for holders of five-star rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wedemeyer_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-wedemeyer-5">[5]</a></sup> In late 1945 and early 1946 he served as a special envoy to China in an unsuccessful effort to negotiate a coalition government between the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Nationalist</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communists</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>.</p>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> from 1947 to 1949, Marshall received credit for the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> for Europe's post-war rebuilding, the success of which was recognized with award of the 1953 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-b-6">[6]</a></sup> After resigning as Secretary of State, Marshall served as chairman of <a href="/wiki/American_Battle_Monuments_Commission" title="American Battle Monuments Commission">American Battle Monuments Commission</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_1949.2C_p._1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_1949.2C_p._1-7">[7]</a></sup> and president of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_National_Red_Cross" title="American National Red Cross">American National Red Cross</a>.</p>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a> at the start of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, Marshall worked to restore the military's confidence and morale at the end of its post-World War II demobilization and then its initial buildup for combat in Korea and operations during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.</p>
<p>After resigning as Defense Secretary, Marshall retired to his home in Virginia. He died in 1959 and was buried at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="1045" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg/220px-1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg/330px-1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg/440px-1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team_marshall_encircled.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team" title="1900 VMI Keydets football team">1900 VMI Keydets football team</a>. Marshall encircled</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>George Catlett Marshall, Jr., was born into a middle-class family in <a href="/wiki/Uniontown,_Pennsylvania" title="Uniontown, Pennsylvania">Uniontown, Pennsylvania</a>, the son of George Catlett Marshall, Sr. and Laura Emily (née Bradford) Marshall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Marshall was a scion of an old <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> family, as well as a distant relative of former <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Marshall graduated from the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Military_Institute" title="Virginia Military Institute">Virginia Military Institute</a> (VMI),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-c_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-c-10">[10]</a></sup> where he was initiated into the <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Alpha_Order" title="Kappa Alpha Order">Kappa Alpha Order</a> in 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> He was an <a href="/wiki/College_Football_All-Southern_Team" title="College Football All-Southern Team">All-Southern</a> <a href="/wiki/Tackle_(gridiron_football_position)" title="Tackle (gridiron football position)">tackle</a> for the <a href="/wiki/VMI_Keydets_football" title="VMI Keydets football">VMI Keydets</a> varsity football team in <a href="/wiki/1900_VMI_Keydets_football_team" title="1900 VMI Keydets football team">1900</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Entry_into_the_Army_and_the_Philippines">Entry into the Army and the Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Entry into the Army and the Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Following graduation from VMI in 1901, Marshall sat for a competitive examination for a commission in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">U.S. Army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> While awaiting the results he took the position of Commandant of Students at the Danville Military Institute in Danville, Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Marshall passed the exam and was commissioned a second lieutenant in February, 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Prior to World War I, he was posted to various positions in the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, including serving as an infantry platoon leader and company commander during the <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">Philippine–American War</a> and other guerrilla uprisings. He was schooled and trained in modern warfare, including a tour at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Leavenworth" title="Fort Leavenworth">Fort Leavenworth</a>, Kansas from 1906 to 1910 as both a student and an instructor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He was the Honor Graduate of his Infantry-Cavalry School Course in 1907, and graduated first in his 1908 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Command_and_General_Staff_College" title="United States Army Command and General Staff College">Army Staff College</a> class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>After another tour of duty in the Philippines, Marshall returned to the United States in 1916 to serve as aide-de-camp to the commander of the Western Department, former Army chief of staff Major General <a href="/wiki/J._Franklin_Bell" title="J. Franklin Bell">J. Franklin Bell</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Presidio" title="Presidio">Presidio</a> in San Francisco. After the United States declared war on Germany in April 1917, Marshall relocated with Bell to <a href="/wiki/Governors_Island" title="Governors Island">Governors Island</a>, New York when Bell was reassigned as commander of the Department of the East. Marshall was soon after assigned to help oversee the mobilization of the <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="1st Infantry Division (United States)">1st Division</a> for service in France.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">Great War</a>, he had roles as a planner of both training and operations. He went to France in mid-1917 as the director of training and planning for the <a href="/wiki/1st_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="1st Infantry Division (United States)">1st Division</a>. In this capacity he planned the first American attack and victory of the war at Cantigny, May 28–31, 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In mid-1918, he was posted to the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/American_Expeditionary_Forces" title="American Expeditionary Forces">American Expeditionary Force</a>, where he worked closely with his mentor, <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">John Joseph Pershing</a>, and was a key planner of American operations. He was instrumental in the planning and coordination of the <a href="/wiki/Meuse-Argonne_Offensive" title="Meuse-Argonne Offensive">Meuse-Argonne Offensive</a>, which contributed to the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/German_Army_(German_Empire)" title="German Army (German Empire)">German Army</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> in 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Between_World_War_I_and_II">Between World War I and II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Between World War I and II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1919, he became an <a href="/wiki/Aide-de-camp" title="Aide-de-camp">aide-de-camp</a> to <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/John_J._Pershing" title="John J. Pershing">John J. Pershing</a>. Between 1920 and 1924, while Pershing was Army Chief of Staff, Marshall worked in a number of positions in the Army, focusing on training and teaching modern, mechanized warfare. Between World Wars I and II, he was a key planner and writer in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_War" title="United States Department of War">War Department</a>, commanded the <a href="/wiki/15th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="15th Infantry Regiment (United States)">15th Infantry Regiment</a> for three years in China, and taught at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_War_College" title="United States Army War College">Army War College</a>. In 1927, as a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_colonel_(United_States)" title="Lieutenant colonel (United States)">lieutenant colonel</a>, he was appointed assistant commandant of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Infantry_School" title="United States Army Infantry School">Infantry School</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Benning" title="Fort Benning">Fort Benning</a>, where he initiated major changes to modernize command and staff processes, which proved to be of major benefit during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Marshall placed <a href="/wiki/Edwin_F._Harding" title="Edwin F. Harding">Edwin F. Harding</a> in charge of the Infantry School's publications, and Harding became editor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:41</sup> of <i>Infantry in Battle</i>, a book that codified the lessons of World War I. <i>Infantry in Battle</i> is still used as an officer's training manual in the Infantry Officer's Course and was the training manual for most of the infantry officers and leaders of World War II.</p>
<p>From June 1932 to June 1933 he was the Commanding Officer of the <a href="/wiki/8th_Infantry_Regiment_(United_States)" title="8th Infantry Regiment (United States)">8th Infantry Regiment</a> at <a href="/wiki/Tybee_Island,_Georgia" title="Tybee Island, Georgia">Fort Screven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>. From July 1933 to October 1933 he was commander of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Moultrie" title="Fort Moultrie">Fort Moultrie</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> and District I of the <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a>, and he was promoted to colonel in September 1933. He was senior instructor and chief of staff for the Illinois National Guard's <a href="/wiki/33rd_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="33rd Infantry Division (United States)">33rd Division</a> from November 1933 to August 1936.</p>
<p>Marshall commanded the 5th Brigade of the <a href="/wiki/3rd_Infantry_Division_(United_States)" title="3rd Infantry Division (United States)">3rd Infantry Division</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vancouver_Barracks" title="Vancouver Barracks">Vancouver Barracks</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver,_Washington" title="Vancouver, Washington">Vancouver,</a> Washington from 1936 to 1938, and was promoted to <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">brigadier general</a> in October 1936. In addition to obtaining a long-sought and significant troop command, traditionally viewed as an indispensable step to the pinnacle of the US Army, Marshall was also responsible for 35 <a href="/wiki/Civilian_Conservation_Corps" title="Civilian Conservation Corps">Civilian Conservation Corps</a> (CCC) camps in Oregon and southern Washington. As post commander Marshall made a concerted effort to cultivate relations with the city of Portland and to enhance the image of the US Army in the region. With the CCC, he initiated a series of measures to improve the morale of the participants and to make the experience beneficial in their later life. He started a newspaper for the CCC region that proved a vehicle to promote CCC successes, and he initiated a variety of programs that developed their skills and improved their health. Marshall's inspections of the CCC camps gave him and his wife Katherine the chance to enjoy the beauty of the American northwest and made that assignment what he called "the most instructive service I ever had, and the most interesting."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 1938, Marshall was assigned to the War Plans Division in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington D.C.</a> and subsequently reassigned as Deputy Chief of Staff. In that capacity, then-Brigadier General Marshall attended a conference at the White House at which <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">President Roosevelt</a> proposed a plan to provide aircraft to England in support of the war effort, lacking forethought with regard to logistical support or training. With all other attendees voicing support of the plan, Marshall was the only person to voice his disagreement. Despite the common belief that he had ended his career, this action resulted in his being nominated by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin Roosevelt</a> to be the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Staff_of_the_United_States_Army" title="Chief of Staff of the United States Army">Army Chief of Staff</a>. Upon the retirement of General <a href="/wiki/Malin_Craig" title="Malin Craig">Malin Craig</a> on July 1, 1939, Marshall became acting chief of staff. Marshall was promoted to <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">general</a> and sworn in as Chief of Staff on September 1, 1939, the same day the <a href="/wiki/German_Army_(Wehrmacht)" title="German Army (Wehrmacht)">German Army</a> launched its <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Poland" title="Invasion of Poland">invasion of Poland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> He would hold this post until the end of the war in 1945.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As Chief of Staff, Marshall organized the largest military expansion in U.S. history, inheriting an outmoded, poorly equipped army of 189,000 men and, partly drawing from his experience teaching and developing techniques of modern warfare as an instructor at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_War_College" title="United States Army War College">Army War College</a>, coordinated the large-scale expansion and modernization of the U.S. Army. Though he had never actually led troops in combat, Marshall was a skilled organizer with a talent for inspiring other officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Many of the American generals who were given top commands during the war were either picked or recommended by Marshall, including <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_L._Devers" title="Jacob L. Devers">Jacob L. Devers</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_S._Patton" title="George S. Patton">George S. Patton</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terry_de_la_Mesa_Allen,_Sr." title="Terry de la Mesa Allen, Sr.">Terry de la Mesa Allen, Sr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Fredendall" title="Lloyd Fredendall">Lloyd Fredendall</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leslie_J._McNair" title="Leslie J. McNair">Leslie McNair</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_Wayne_Clark" title="Mark Wayne Clark">Mark Wayne Clark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bradley" title="Omar Bradley">Omar Bradley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expands_military_force_fortyfold">Expands military force fortyfold</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Expands military force fortyfold">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Faced with the necessity of turning an army of former civilians into a force of over eight million soldiers by 1942 (a fortyfold increase within three years), Marshall directed General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leslie_J._McNair" title="Leslie J. McNair">Leslie McNair</a> to focus efforts on rapidly producing large numbers of soldiers. With the exception of airborne forces, Marshall approved McNair's concept of an abbreviated training schedule for men entering Army land forces training, particularly in regard to basic infantry skills, weapons proficiency, and combat tactics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> At the time, most U.S. commanders at lower levels had little or no combat experience of any kind. Without the input of experienced British or Allied combat officers on the nature of modern warfare and enemy tactics, many resorted to formulaic training methods emphasizing static defense and orderly large-scale advances by motorized convoys over improved roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In consequence, Army forces deploying to Africa suffered serious initial reverses when encountering German armored combat units in Africa at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kasserine_Pass" title="Kasserine Pass">Kasserine Pass</a> and other major battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-29">[29]</a></sup> Even as late as 1944, U.S. soldiers undergoing stateside training in preparation for deployment against German forces in Europe were not being trained in combat procedures and tactics in use there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Replacement_system_criticized">Replacement system criticized</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Replacement system criticized">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="332" data-file-width="436" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg/220px-George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg/330px-George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_marshall%26henry_stimson.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marshall with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_Stimson" title="Henry Stimson">Henry Stimson</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Originally, Marshall had planned a 265-division <a href="/wiki/Army" title="Army">Army</a> with a system of unit rotation such as practiced by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">British</a> and other Allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Article_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Article-31">[31]</a></sup> By mid-1943, however, after pressure from government and business leaders to preserve manpower for industry and agriculture, he had abandoned this plan in favor of a 90-division Army using individual replacements sent via a circuitous process from training to divisions in combat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Article_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Article-31">[31]</a></sup> The individual replacement system devised by Marshall and implemented by McNair greatly exacerbated problems with <a href="/wiki/Unit_cohesion" title="Unit cohesion">unit cohesion</a> and effective transfer of combat experience to newly trained soldiers and officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrose.2C_Stephen_pp._277-284_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose.2C_Stephen_pp._277-284-32">[32]</a></sup> In Europe, where there were few pauses in combat with German forces, the individual replacement system had broken down completely by late 1944.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Hastily trained replacements or service personnel reassigned as infantry were given six weeks' refresher training and thrown into battle with Army divisions locked in front-line combat.</p>
<p>The new men were often not even proficient in the use of their own rifles or weapons systems, and once in combat, could not receive enough practical instruction from veterans before being killed or wounded, usually within the first three or four days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry.2C_Mark_R._2001_pp._12-14_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henry.2C_Mark_R._2001_pp._12-14-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Under such conditions, many replacements suffered a crippling loss of morale, while veteran soldiers were kept in line units until they were killed, wounded, or incapacitated by battle fatigue or physical illness. Incidents of soldiers AWOL from combat duty as well as battle fatigue and self-inflicted injury rose rapidly during the last eight months of the war with Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrose.2C_Stephen_pp._277-284_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose.2C_Stephen_pp._277-284-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry.2C_Mark_R._2001_pp._12-14_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Henry.2C_Mark_R._2001_pp._12-14-34">[34]</a></sup> As one historian concluded, "Had the Germans been given a free hand to devise a replacement system..., one that would do the Americans the most harm and the least good, they could not have done a better job."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henry.2C_Mark_R._2001_pp._12-14_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Henry.2C_Mark_R._2001_pp._12-14-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Marshall's abilities to pick competent field commanders during the early part of the war was decidedly mixed. While he had been instrumental in advancing the career of the able Dwight D. Eisenhower, he had also recommended the swaggering <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Fredendall" title="Lloyd Fredendall">Lloyd Fredendall</a> to Eisenhower for a major command in the American invasion of North Africa during <a href="/wiki/Operation_Torch" title="Operation Torch">Operation <i>Torch</i></a>. Marshall was especially fond of Fredendall, describing him as "one of the best" and remarking in a staff meeting when his name was mentioned, "I like that man; you can see determination all over his face." Eisenhower duly picked him to command the 39,000-man Central Task Force (the largest of three) in Operation <i>Torch</i>. Both men would come to regret that decision, as Fredendall was the leader of U.S. Army forces at the disastrous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Kasserine_Pass" title="Battle of the Kasserine Pass">Battle of the Kasserine Pass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planned_invasion_of_Europe">Planned invasion of Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Planned invasion of Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="363" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif/220px-Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif/330px-Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Infantry_in_battle_cover.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cover to the book <i>Infantry in Battle</i>, the World War II officer's guide to infantry combat operations. Marshall directed production of the book, which is still used as a reference today.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During World War II, Marshall was instrumental in preparing the U.S. Army and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">Army Air Forces</a> for the invasion of the European continent. Marshall wrote the document that would become the central strategy for all Allied operations in Europe. He initially scheduled <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">Operation <i>Overlord</i></a> for April 1, 1943, but met with strong opposition from Winston Churchill, who convinced Roosevelt to commit troops to Operation <i>Husky</i> for the invasion of Italy. Some authors think that World War II could have ended one year earlier if Marshall had had his way; others think that such an invasion would have meant utter failure.</p>
<p>It was assumed that Marshall would become the Supreme Commander of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Overlord" title="Operation Overlord">Operation <i>Overlord</i></a>, but Roosevelt selected <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> as Supreme Commander. While Marshall enjoyed considerable success in working with Congress and President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, he refused to lobby for the position. President Roosevelt didn't want to lose his presence in the states. He told Marshall, "I didn't feel I could sleep at ease if you were out of Washington."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-d-37">[37]</a></sup> When rumors circulated that the top job would go to Marshall, many critics viewed the transfer as a demotion for Marshall, since he would leave his position as Chief of Staff of the Army and lose his seat on the <a href="/wiki/Combined_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Combined Chiefs of Staff">Combined Chiefs of Staff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 16, 1944, Marshall became the first American Army general to be promoted to <a href="/wiki/Five-star_rank" title="Five-star rank">five-star rank</a>, the newly created <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)#World_War_II_era" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a> – the American equivalent rank to <a href="/wiki/Field_marshal" title="Field marshal">field marshal</a>. He was the second American to be promoted to a five-star rank, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Daniel_Leahy" title="William Daniel Leahy">William Leahy</a> was promoted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fleet_Admiral_(United_States)" title="Fleet Admiral (United States)">fleet admiral</a> the previous day.</p>
<p>Throughout the remainder of World War II, Marshall coordinated Allied operations in Europe and the Pacific. He was characterized as the organizer of Allied victory by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine named Marshall <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_Magazine_Person_of_the_Year" title="Time Magazine Person of the Year">Man of the Year</a> for 1943. Marshall resigned his post of Chief of Staff in 1945, but did not retire, as regulations stipulate that Generals of the Army remain on active duty for life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wedemeyer_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-wedemeyer-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Analysis_of_Pearl_Harbor_intelligence_failure">Analysis of Pearl Harbor intelligence failure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Analysis of Pearl Harbor intelligence failure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After World War II ended, the Congressional Joint Committee on the Investigation of the <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor Attack</a> received testimony on the intelligence failure. It amassed 25,000 pages of documents, 40 volumes, and included nine reports and investigations, eight of which had been previously completed. These reports included criticism of Marshall for delay in sending General <a href="/wiki/Walter_Short" title="Walter Short">Walter Short</a>, the Army commander in Hawaii, important information obtained from intercepted Japanese diplomatic messages. The report also criticized Marshall's lack of knowledge of the readiness of the Hawaiian Command during November and December 1941. Ten days after the attack, Lt. General Short and Admiral <a href="/wiki/Husband_E._Kimmel" title="Husband E. Kimmel">Husband E. Kimmel</a>, commander of the Navy at Pearl Harbor, were both relieved of their duties. The final report of the Joint Committee did not single out or fault Marshall. While the report was critical of the overall situation, the committee noted that subordinates had failed to pass on important information to their superiors, including Marshall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post_War:_China">Post War: China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Post War: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In December 1945, President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harry_Truman" title="Harry Truman">Harry Truman</a> <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Mission" title="Marshall Mission">sent Marshall</a> to China, to broker a coalition government between the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Nationalist</a> allies under Generalissimo <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communists</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>. Marshall had no leverage over the Communists, but he threatened to withdraw American aid essential to the Nationalists. Both sides rejected his proposals and the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> escalated, with the Communists winning in 1949. His mission a failure, he returned to the United States in January 1947.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-f_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-f-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-g_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-g-42">[42]</a></sup> Chiang Kai-shek and some historians later claimed that cease-fire, under pressure of Marshall, saved the Communists from defeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanner2013_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanner2013-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> As Secretary of State in 1947–48, Marshall seems to have disagreed with strong opinions in <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">The Pentagon</a> and State Department that Chiang's success was vital to American interests, insisting that U.S. troops not become involved.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State_and_Nobel_Peace_Prize">Secretary of State and Nobel Peace Prize</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Secretary of State and Nobel Peace Prize">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="782" data-file-width="1660" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg/220px-MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg/330px-MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg/440px-MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MedalGeorgeMarshall1982.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Medallion issued in 1982 to honor George Marshall's post-war work for Europe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Marshall's return to the U.S. in early 1947, Truman appointed Marshall <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>. He became the spokesman for the State Department's ambitious plans to rebuild Europe. On June 5, 1947 in a speech<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, he outlined the American proposal. The European Recovery Program, as it was formally known, became known as the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>. <a href="/wiki/Clark_Clifford" title="Clark Clifford">Clark Clifford</a> had suggested to Truman that the plan be called the Truman Plan, but Truman immediately dismissed that idea and insisted that it be called the Marshall Plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcculloughtrumanbook_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-mcculloughtrumanbook-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Marshall Plan would help Europe quickly rebuild and modernize its economy along American lines. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> forbade its <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state" title="Satellite state">satellites</a> to participate.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:George_Marshall_1947.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2432" data-file-width="1904" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/George_Marshall_1947.jpg/220px-George_Marshall_1947.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/George_Marshall_1947.jpg/330px-George_Marshall_1947.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/George_Marshall_1947.jpg/440px-George_Marshall_1947.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_Marshall_1947.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marshall during World War II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marshall was again named <i>Time'</i>s Man of the Year for 1947. He received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for his post-war work in 1953, the only career officer in United States Army to ever receive this honor.</p>
<p>As Secretary of State, Marshall strongly opposed recognizing the state of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. Marshall felt that if the state of Israel was declared that a war would break out in the Middle East (which it did in 1948 one day after Israel declared independence). Marshall saw recognizing the Jewish state as a political move to gain Jewish support in the upcoming election, in which Truman was expected to lose to Dewey. He told President Truman in May 1948, "If you (recognize the state of Israel) and if I were to vote in the election, I would vote against you."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> However, Marshall refused to vote in any election as a matter of principle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Marshall resigned from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">State Department</a> because of ill health on January 7, 1949, and the same month became chairman of the <a href="/wiki/American_Battle_Monuments_Commission" title="American Battle Monuments Commission">American Battle Monuments Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_York_Times_1949.2C_p._1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-New_York_Times_1949.2C_p._1-7">[7]</a></sup> In September 1949, Marshall was named president of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_National_Red_Cross" title="American National Red Cross">American National Red Cross</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_Defense">Secretary of Defense</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Secretary of Defense">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When the early months of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> showed how poorly prepared the Defense Department was, President Truman fired Secretary <a href="/wiki/Louis_A._Johnson" title="Louis A. Johnson">Louis A. Johnson</a> and named Marshall as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">Secretary of Defense</a> in September 1950. The appointment required a congressional waiver because the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Act_of_1947" title="National Security Act of 1947">National Security Act of 1947</a> prohibited a uniformed military officer from serving in the post. This prohibition included Marshall since individuals promoted to <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a> are not technically retired, but remain officially on active duty even after their active service has concluded. Marshall's main role as Secretary of Defense was to restore confidence and morale while rebuilding the armed forces following their post-World War II demobilization.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_War">Korean War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Korean War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8602" data-file-width="6829" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Edgar_Stephens_-_George_C._Marshall_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
George Marshall portrait by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Stephens" title="Thomas E. Stephens">Thomas E. Stephens</a> (c. 1949).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marshall worked to provide more manpower to meet the demands of both the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> in Europe. To implement his priorities Marshall brought in a new leadership team, including <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Lovett" title="Robert A. Lovett">Robert A. Lovett</a> as his deputy and <a href="/wiki/Anna_M._Rosenberg" title="Anna M. Rosenberg">Anna M. Rosenberg</a>, former head of the <a href="/wiki/War_Manpower_Commission" title="War Manpower Commission">War Manpower Commission</a>, as assistant secretary of defense for manpower. He also worked to rebuild the relationship between the Defense and State Departments, as well as the relationship between the Secretary of Defense and the Joint Chiefs of Staff.</p>
<p>Marshall participated in the post-<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Inchon" title="Battle of Inchon">Inchon landing</a> discussion that led to authorizing <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> to conduct operations in North Korea. A secret "eyes only" signal from Marshall to MacArthur on September 29, 1950 declared the Truman administration's commitment: "We want you to feel unhampered strategically and tactically to proceed north of the <a href="/wiki/38th_parallel_north" title="38th parallel north">38th Parallel</a>". At the same time, Marshall advised against public pronouncements which might lead to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> votes undermining or countermanding the initial mandate to restore the border between North and South Korea. Marshall and the Joint Chiefs of Staff were generally supportive of MacArthur because they were of the view that field commanders should be able to exercise their best judgment in accomplishing the intent of their superiors.</p>
<p>Following Chinese military intervention in Korea during late November, Marshall and the Joint Chiefs of Staff sought ways to aid MacArthur while avoiding all-out war with China. In the debate over what to do about China's increased involvement, Marshall opposed a cease–fire on the grounds that it would make the U.S. look weak in China's eyes, leading to demands for future concessions. In addition, Marshall argued that the U.S. had a moral obligation to honor its commitment to South Korea. When British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> suggested diplomatic overtures to China, Marshall opposed, arguing that it was impossible to negotiate with the Communist government. In addition, Marshall expressed concern that concessions to China would undermine confidence in the U.S. among its Asian allies, including Japan and the Philippines. When some in Congress favored expanding the war in Korea and confronting China, Marshall argued against a wider war in Korea, continuing instead to stress the importance of containing the Soviet Union during the Cold War battle for primacy in Europe.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Relief_of_General_MacArthur">Relief of General MacArthur</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Relief of General MacArthur">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/President_Truman%27s_relief_of_General_Douglas_MacArthur" title="President Truman's relief of General Douglas MacArthur">President Truman's relief of General Douglas MacArthur</a></div>
<p>Increasingly concerned about public statements from General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>, commander of United Nations forces fighting in the Korean War, which contradicted President <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>'s on prosecution of the war, on the morning of 6 April 1951 Truman held a meeting with Marshall, <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> Omar Bradley, Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Dean_Acheson" title="Dean Acheson">Acheson</a> and advisor <a href="/wiki/W._Averell_Harriman" title="W. Averell Harriman">W. Averell Harriman</a> to discuss whether MacArthur should be removed from command.</p>
<p>Harriman was emphatically in favor of MacArthur's relief, but Bradley opposed it. Marshall asked for more time to consider the matter. Acheson was in favor but did not disclose this, instead warning Truman that if he did it, MacArthur's relief would cause "the biggest fight of your administration." At another meeting the following day, Marshall and Bradley continued to oppose MacArthur's relief. On 8 April, the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> met with Marshall, and each expressed the view that MacArthur's relief was desirable from a "military point of view," suggesting that "if MacArthur were not relieved, a large segment of our people would charge that civil authorities no longer controlled the military."</p>
<p>Marshall, Bradley, Acheson and Harriman met with Truman again on 9 April. Bradley informed the President of the views of the Joint Chiefs, and Marshall added that he agreed with them. Truman wrote in his diary that "it is of unanimous opinion of all that MacArthur be relieved. All four so advise."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> (The Joint Chiefs would later insist that they had only "concurred" with the relief, not "recommended" it.)</p>
<p>On April 11, 1951, President Truman directed transmittal of an order to MacArthur, issued over Bradley's signature, relieving MacArthur of his assignment in Korea and directing him to turn over command to <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Ridgway" title="Matthew Ridgway">Matthew Ridgway</a>. In line with Marshall's view, and those of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, MacArthur's relief was looked upon by proponents as being necessary to reassert the tenet of <a href="/wiki/Civilian_control_of_the_military" title="Civilian control of the military">civilian control of the military</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Marshall retired in September 1951 to his home, <a href="/wiki/The_Marshall_House" title="The Marshall House">Dodona Manor</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Leesburg,_Virginia" title="Leesburg, Virginia">Leesburg, Virginia</a> to tend to his gardens and continue his passion for horseback riding. He was head of the American delegation at <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Queen_Elizabeth_II" title="Coronation of Queen Elizabeth II">the coronation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen Elizabeth II</a> in 1953. He also served as Chairman of the <a href="/wiki/American_Battle_Monuments_Commission" title="American Battle Monuments Commission">American Battle Monuments Commission</a> from 1949 to 1959.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_burial">Death and burial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Death and burial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1011" data-file-width="778" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg/220px-George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg/330px-George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg/440px-George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:George_Marshall_Gravesite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gravesite of George Marshall at Arlington National Cemetery</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marshall died at <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Medical_Center" title="Walter Reed Army Medical Center">Walter Reed Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a> on October 16, 1959 at the age of 78. He is interred at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" title="Arlington, Virginia">Arlington, Virginia</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reputation_and_legacy">Reputation and legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Reputation and legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Marshall's reputation for excellence as a military organizer and planner was recognized early in his career, and became known throughout the Army. In a performance appraisal prepared while Marshall was a lieutenant in the Philippines, his superior, Captain E. J. Williams responded to the routine question of whether he would want the evaluated officer to serve under his command again by writing of Marshall "Should the exigencies of active service place him in exalted command I would be glad to serve <i>under</i> him." (Emphasis added.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1913 General <a href="/wiki/Johnson_Hagood_(general)" title="Johnson Hagood (general)">Johnson Hagood</a>, then a lieutenant colonel, completed a written evaluation of Marshall's performance in which he called Marshall a military genius. Responding to the question of whether he would want his subordinate Marshall to serve under him again, Hagood wrote "Yes, but I would prefer to serve <i>under</i> his command." (Emphasis added.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to his military success, Marshall is primarily remembered as the driving force behind the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, which provided billions in aid to post war Europe to restart the economies of the destroyed countries. In recent years, the cooperation required between former European adversaries as part of the Marshall Plan has been recognized as one of the earliest factors that led to formation of the <a href="/wiki/European_Coal_and_Steel_Community" title="European Coal and Steel Community">European Coal and Steel Community</a>, and eventually the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a television interview after leaving office, <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> was asked which American he thought had made the greatest contribution of the preceding thirty years. Without hesitation, Truman picked Marshall, adding "I don't think in this age in which I have lived, that there has been a man who has been a greater administrator; a man with a knowledge of military affairs equal to General Marshall."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a> said in an interview with <a href="/wiki/Dick_Cavett" title="Dick Cavett">Dick Cavett</a> that "Marshall is the greatest man I ever met... I think he was the greatest human being who was also a great man... He was a tremendous gentleman, an old fashioned institution which isn't with us anymore."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_life">Family life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Family life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Marshall married Elizabeth Carter Coles, or "Lily", on Letcher Avenue at her mother's home in Lexington, Virginia, in 1902. She died in 1927 after a successful surgery that put significant strain on her weak heart. They did not have children.</p>
<p>In 1930, Marshall married Katherine Boyce Tupper (October 8, 1882 – December 18, 1978), widow of <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a> lawyer Clifton Stevenson Brown, who was murdered by a disgruntled client, and the mother of three children. One of Marshall's stepsons with Tupper was US Army Lieutenant Allen Tupper Brown, who was killed by a German sniper in Italy on May 29, 1944. Another stepson was Major Clifton Stevenson Brown, Jr. (1914–1952). Step-daughter Molly Brown Winn, who is the mother of actress <a href="/wiki/Kitty_Winn" title="Kitty Winn">Kitty Winn</a>, was married to US Army Major James J. Winn (former aide to General Marshall).</p>
<p>Marshall was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freemason" title="Freemason">Freemason</a>, having been made a Mason "at sight" in 1941 by the Grand Master of the Grand Lodge of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>George Marshall maintained a home, known as Dodona Manor and later as <a href="/wiki/The_Marshall_House" title="The Marshall House">The Marshall House</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Leesburg,_Virginia" title="Leesburg, Virginia">Leesburg, Virginia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This was his first and only permanent residence owned by Marshall who later said "this is Home...a real home after 41 years of wandering."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The restored home and its surrounding gardens are open to the public as a museum.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictional_portrayals">Fictional portrayals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fictional portrayals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Marshall has played in film and television by</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Keith_Andes" title="Keith Andes">Keith Andes</a> in the 1970 film <i><a href="/wiki/Tora!_Tora!_Tora!" title="Tora! Tora! Tora!">Tora! Tora! Tora!</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ward_Costello" title="Ward Costello">Ward Costello</a> in the 1977 film <a href="/wiki/MacArthur_(film)" title="MacArthur (film)"><i>MacArthur</i></a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dana_Andrews" title="Dana Andrews">Dana Andrews</a> in the 1979 film <i>Ike, The War Years</i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Norman_Burton" title="Norman Burton">Norman Burton</a> in the 1988 miniseries <a href="/wiki/War_and_Remembrance_(miniseries)" title="War and Remembrance (miniseries)"><i>War and Remembrance</i></a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hal_Holbrook" title="Hal Holbrook">Hal Holbrook</a> in the 1989 television film <a href="/wiki/Day_One_(1989_film)" title="Day One (1989 film)"><i>Day One</i></a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harris_Yulin" title="Harris Yulin">Harris Yulin</a> in the 1995 television movie <a href="/wiki/Truman_(1995_film)" title="Truman (1995 film)"><i>Truman</i></a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harve_Presnell" title="Harve Presnell">Harve Presnell</a> in the 1998 film <i><a href="/wiki/Saving_Private_Ryan" title="Saving Private Ryan">Saving Private Ryan</a></i>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scott_Wilson_(actor)" title="Scott Wilson (actor)">Scott Wilson</a> in the 2001 film <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor_(film)" title="Pearl Harbor (film)"><i>Pearl Harbor</i></a>.</li>
<li>Donald Eugene McCoy in the 2009 Chinese movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Founding_of_a_Republic" title="The Founding of a Republic">The Founding of a Republic</a></i>.</li>
<li>Richard DuVal in the 2012 Russian mini-series "Chkalov".</li>
</ul>
<p>Marshall is a character in three different <a href="/wiki/Alternate_history" title="Alternate history">alternate history</a> timelines in novels by <a href="/wiki/Harry_Turtledove" title="Harry Turtledove">Harry Turtledove</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worldwar" title="Worldwar">Worldwar</a>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joe_Steele" title="Joe Steele">Joe Steele</a></i>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=The_Hot_War&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="The Hot War (page does not exist)">The Hot War</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian_honors">Civilian honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Marshall&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Civilian honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In 1946, he was awarded the United States Congressional Gold Medal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1948, he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Lodge_of_New_York" title="Grand Lodge of New York">Grand Lodge of New York</a>'s Distinguished Achievement Award for his role and contributions during and after World War II.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> 1953 for the Marshall Plan.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> honored him with a <a href="/wiki/Prominent_Americans_series" title="Prominent Americans series">Prominent Americans series</a> (1965–1978) 20¢ postage stamp.</li>
<li>1959 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charlemagne_Award" title="Charlemagne Award">Karlspreis (International Charlemagne Prize of the city of Aachen)</a>.</li>
<li>1960 George C. <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a>, originally the Army Ballistics Missile Agency at Redstone Arsenal, Huntsville Alabama, became a NASA field center and was renamed.</li>
<li>The British Parliament established the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Scholarship" title="Marshall Scholarship">Marshall Scholarship</a> in recognition of Marshall's contributions to Anglo-American relations.</li>
<li>Many buildings and streets throughout the U.S. and other nations are named in his honor.</li>
<li>George C. Marshall Award, the highest award given to a chapter in Kappa Alpha Order.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_C._Marshall_High_School" title="George C. Marshall High School">George C. Marshall High School</a>, founded in 1962 and located in <a href="/wiki/Falls_Church,_Virginia" title="Falls Church, Virginia">Falls Church, Virginia</a>, is the only public high school in the United States named for Marshall. The nickname of the school – "The Statesmen" – appropriately reflects his life and contributions.</li>
<li>The Marshall Elementary School is in the Laurel Highlands School District, Uniontown, Pennsylvania.</li>
<li>George C. Marshall Elementary School: located in Vancouver, Washington.</li>
<li>The George C. Marshall European Center for Security Studies in Garmisch-Partenkirchen, Germany</li>
<li>George Catlett Marshall Medal, awarded by the Association of the United States Army. Awarded to <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a> in 1972.</li>
</ul>
<p><a class="external free" href="http://marshallfoundation.org/library/collection/pogue-biographies/volume1-education-of-a-general/#!/collection=272" rel="nofollow">http://marshallfoundation.org/library/collection/pogue-biographies/volume1-education-of-a-general/#!/collection=272</a></p>

<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

<li><i>The Papers of George Catlett Marshall:</i> (Larry I. Bland and Sharon Ritenour Stevens, eds.)
<ul>

<li>Vol. 5: "The Finest Soldier," January 1, 1945 – January 7, 1947. (2003)</li>
<li>Vol. 6: "The Whole World Hangs in the Balance," January 8, 1947 – September 30, 1949. (2012)</li>

</ul>
</li>
